        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.2c07897"><meta property="og:title" content="Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na3V2(PO4)2F3 Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/acsami.2c07897.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Na3V2(PO4)2F3 is one of the most studied polyanion type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and offers great promises. However, the inferior rate capability induced by its sluggish diffusion of electrons and ions greatly limits the practical application of electrode materials in SIBs. Herein, we develop an efficient method to fabricate in situ carbon-coated Na3V2(PO4)2F3 nanosheets by using cost-effective amylopectin. The amylopectin not only could induce the nucleation of Na3V2(PO4)2F3 along its backbone to form a 2D nanostructure, but also act as a source of amorphous carbon for in situ coating on the active material surface. The composite exhibits extraordinary rate capability (104 mA h g–1 at 40 C, 51 mA h g–1 at 150 C) and desirable cycling stability. Such satisfactory achievements, especially the superior rate performance, should be ascribed to its unique 2D nanostructure which shortens the Na+ diffusion length, and the in situ carbon coating endows the composites with effective electron transport. Even applied to full cells, the obtained devices still display an exceptionally high energy density (94.8 W h kg–1), high power density (7295 W kg–1), and excellent cyclic stability."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/acsami.2c07897.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931881e25a2a54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931881e25a2a54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'c87047b2-68cc-4322-ad8e-9227d67631c4'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/article-dc83549a302b9ecf025f.js"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/lazy-imports-6922fe6a4ca6b2e0cd9a.js"></script><script type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931881e25a2a54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931881e25a2a54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Amylopectin-Assisted Fabrication of In Situ Carbon-Coated Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanosheets for Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c07897", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

                   <span>Abstract</span>

            <li>
                <a title="1.  Introduction" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#_i2" data-id="article_header_JumpTo_1.%20%20Introduction">
                   <span>1.  Introduction</span>
                </a>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">19</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07897&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> is one of the most studied polyanion type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and offers great promises. However, the inferior rate capability induced by its sluggish diffusion of electrons and ions greatly limits the practical application of electrode materials in SIBs. Herein, we develop an efficient method to fabricate in situ carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> nanosheets by using cost-effective amylopectin. The amylopectin not only could induce the nucleation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> along its backbone to form a 2D nanostructure, but also act as a source of amorphous carbon for in situ coating on the active material surface. The composite exhibits extraordinary rate capability (104 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 40 C, 51 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 150 C) and desirable cycling stability. Such satisfactory achievements, especially the superior rate performance, should be ascribed to its unique 2D nanostructure which shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion length, and the in situ carbon coating endows the composites with effective electron transport. Even applied to full cells, the obtained devices still display an exceptionally high energy density (94.8 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>), high power density (7295 W kg<sup>–1</sup>), and excellent cyclic stability.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D40812%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYue%2BZhang%252C%2BWenjun%2BSong%252C%2BYakun%2BTang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D36%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c07897%26title%3DAmylopectin-Assisted%2BFabrication%2Bof%2BIn%2BSitu%2BCarbon-Coated%2BNa3V2%2528PO4%25292F3%2BNanosheets%2Bfor%2BUltra-Fast%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D40821%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na3v22f3&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na3V2%28PO4%292F3">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanosheets&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanosheets">nanosheets</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20batteries">Na-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Rate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20rate">high rate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Power+Density&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_power%20density">power density</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Owning to the advantages of high operating voltage, long cycle life, high energy density and wide applicable temperature range, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have become an ideal choice for portable electronic devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, the high cost of lithium resulting from limited lithium resources leads to a crucial limitation of LIBs for large-scale applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received a lot of attention, due to abundant, widely distributed sodium resources and the potential of lower-cost batteries for large-scale energy-storage applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> However, due to heavier sodium ions, SIBs cannot compete with LIBs in terms of energy density. Therefore, the urgent problem of SIBs is to improve the energy density, especially the cathode material which is the limiting component in terms of operating voltage and specific capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among the various cathode materials like layered oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8−10)</a> Prussian blue analogues, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> and organic cathodes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> polyanions have been intensively investigated due to their 3D framework feature and a small volume change during sodium intercalation/deintercalation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Particularly, NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) has been considered one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs. Because of its energy density (∼507 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>) and theoretical capacity (128 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), which is an important milestone to approach the performance of commercial LIB cathodes (LiFePO<sub>4</sub>, 580 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>; LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, 480 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> Nevertheless, the intrinsic poor electronic conductivity (10<sup>–12</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>) and sluggish electrochemical kinetics-induced worse rate performance are primary problems impeding its further development. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> Especially, the rate performance is a crucial indicator for large-scale application of batteries. Intensive efforts have been dedicated to developing strategies for facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> migration and electron transfer to improve rate performance. Li et al. improved the kinetics of NVPF through uniformly compositing with reduced graphene oxide, and the composite delivered a high capacity of 59 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 70 C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Hu et al. successfully prepared spherical Br-doped NVPF/C by a one-step spray drying technology, and the unique spherical cathode exhibits a moderate capacity at a high rate (95.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 C). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Li et al. improved the intrinsic ion/electronic conductivity of NVPF through Fe-substitution in NVPF/C on the vanadium site for high power SIBs, achieving a discharge capacity of 47.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 80 C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> However, due to the low sodium bulk mobility in feature, it is rare to get the reported results of ultrahigh rate performances (above 100 C, charge and discharge less than 36 s), which is great significance to realize the rapid charging and discharge of energy-storage applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, 2D nanostructures with an ultrathin layered structure and infinite planar length have aroused the interest of researchers in energy storage, especially fast Li<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> migration with the following advantages. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> First, its relatively large specific surface can provide abundant electrochemically active sites and short Na<sup>+</sup> transport pathways. Second, its open layered structure is beneficial for the access of electrolyte ions and reduces volume change. However, the preparation of nanostructured NVPF with complicated multicomponent is a big challenge because of particle growth and aggregation during high-temperature treatment, let alone constructing a 2D nanostructure. Coating the inorganic sources (such as graphene, carbon nanotubes, and mesoporous carbon CMK-3) on the surfaces of NVPF is a facile way of enhancing the extrinsic electronic conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 to 25">(23−25)</a> However, an inefficient electronic contact between these two components and their uneven distribution may lead to a limited improvement in the electrochemical performance. In addition, these commonly used inorganic sources are relatively expensive, which is less favorable for practical applications. Low-cost organic carbon sources should be considered to modify NVPF, while the homogeneity, thickness, and coverage rate of pyrolytic carbon should also be taken into account. The selection of organic carbon sources becomes critical for achieving a homogeneous carbon-coating layer. Therefore, it is still a challenge to develop a simple, effective, and economical method to synthesize NVPF/C with a 2D nanostructure and satisfactory carbon coating. A satisfactory structural model is one in which carbon is in situ coated on NVPF nanosheets. Nevertheless, to the best of our knowledge, such a satisfactory structure has not been reported for NVPF cathodes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here, in situ carbon-coated NVPF nanosheets (NVPFC-NS) were prepared by using cost-effective amylopectin as a carbon source and structure direction agent. The obtained nanosheets showed excellent cycling stability and impressively high rate performance (93 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 60 C, 51 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 150 C). The extraordinary performance of NVPFC-NS can be associated with its unique 2D nanostructure and in situ carbon coating. Furthermore, to achieve real applications of NVPFC-NS cathode, it also can be paired up to fabricate full cells, which presents satisfactory full-cell performances, suggesting its potential practical sodium storage applications.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Preparation of NVPF/C-NS</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All reagents were purchased from commercial suppliers and used without further purification. First, 0.25 g of amylopectin (Sigma-Aldrich) and 0.25 g of urea (Aladdin) was initially put into 50 mL of distilled water under vigorous agitation for 10 min, then stoichiometric amounts of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin), NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (Aladdin), and NaF (Aladdin) were sequentially dissolved into the solution under agitation for 20 min, the obtained yellow turbid solution was poured into a 100 mL Teflon-lined autoclave and kept in an electric oven at 80 °C for 4 h. The resulting yellow transparent solution was frozen by a vacuum drying process for about 24 h. Then, the dried powder was annealed at 350 °C for 3 h and then at 650 °C for 8 h under an Ar atmosphere to yield the final product of NVPFC-NS. For comparison, a control experiment was also performed, the other experimental steps are the same, except that amylopectin was replaced with oxalate, and the obtained product was named NVPF-NP.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Crystallographic information of the products was recorded by X-ray diffraction (XRD) under a Bruker D8 diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation. The morphology of the composites was analyzed through field-emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM, JEOL-JSM7500), high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM, G2F-20, FEI Tecnai), and energy dispersive spectroscopy mapping. Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy was measured under a JASCO FTIR 4100 spectrometer. Thermogravimetric analysis was performed with a Netzsch STA 449C in air at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. Nitrogen adsorption/desorption (ASAP 2020, Micromeritics, America) was performed to test specific surface area, estimated by Brunauer–Emmet–Teller (BET) method, and the pore size distribution was calculated by the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method. The electronic state of elements was investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Axis Ultra DLD, Kratos Analytical).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The slurry of working electrodes was made up of 75 wt % active materials, 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride, and 15 wt % Ketjen Black with <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) as the solvent. Al foils as collectors are covered with mixed slurry. Then, the prepared electrodes were dried in a vacuum oven at 100 °C for 12 h. The working electrodes were punched out with diameters of 12 mm, and mass loading of 1.5–2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. CR2025-type coin cells were used to measure the electrochemical performances of all the samples. The electrolyte consisted of 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (EC/PC, 1:1 in volume) with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate, and the separator is Whatman 934-AH glass microfibers. All the electrochemical tests were tested at 25 °C. Galvanostatic charge/discharge and cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed in a potential range of 2.3–4.5 V for cathodes. The specific capacity was calculated based on the total mass of the composites. The energy and power density of full cells were calculated with the following equations<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>I</i> is the constant current density (A g<sup>–1</sup>), <i>t</i> is the discharge time (s), <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> are the start and the end times of discharge (s) of full cells, respectively, and <i>V</i> is the working voltage (V).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The schematic illustration of the preparation of NVPFC-NS is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. Briefly, urea, amylopectin, and NVPF precursors are added into deionized water forming a turbid solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>I), because both the NVPF precursor of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> and amylopectin are all insoluble. After low-temperature hydrothermal treatment, the urea is hydrolyzed and releases ammonia, and then reacts with the hydroxyl along the polymer chain of amylopectin to form amino by hydroxy ammonolysis, turning the amylopectin from hydrophobic to hydrophilic; in addition, NVPF precursor (NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub>) is also soluble in ammonia water, in good agreement with the final obtained transparent solution shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>II. Due to the redox reaction between oxidant of the NVPF precursor (NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub>) and reductant of amino along the polymer chain of modified amylopectin, NVPF tends to nucleate along the polymer chain of amylopectin to form 2D nanosheets after heat treatment; meanwhile, pyrolytic carbon derived from amylopectin is in situ coated on the nanosheets.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the preparation of NVPFC-NS.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07897&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The XRD patterns of NVPFC-NS and NVPF-NP were collected and plotted, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a. All diffraction peaks can be unambiguously assigned to a single-phase of NVPF, consistent with the JCPDS data (PDF#01-089-8485) and the reported literature without carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> speculating that the small amount of carbon has a rare impact on the phase structure of NVPF. The carbon contents of both samples are further tested by thermogravimetry (TG) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). There is almost no carbon in NVPF-NP, indicating the carbon derived from pyrolytic oxalic acid is only enough to reduce the precursors in the calcining process, and no extra carbon is left in the final product. While NVPFC-NS had a modest carbon content of 6 wt % derived from pyrolytic amylopectin. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, the Raman spectrum of the NVPFC-NS sample shows obvious characteristic peaks of carbonaceous materials at ≈1356 (D band) and 1579 cm<sup>–1</sup> (G band), suggesting the existence of carbon in the composite. A much higher intensity ratio of D versus G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> = 1.06) compared with that of ordered synthetic graphite (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> = 0.09) demonstrates a higher degree of disorder for the carbon present in the coated NVPF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> However, only a few bands in the range of 250–1100 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be detected in NVPF-NP, verifying again that almost no carbon is left in this sample.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns, (b) TG curves, and (c) Raman spectra of NVPFC-NS and NVPF-NP. XPS spectrum of (d) N 1s and (e) C 1s of NVPFC-NS. (f) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of NVPFC-NS and NVPF-NP.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07897&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">XPS was used to further understand the chemical compositions of the carbon in NVPFC-NS. The N 1s spectrum can be deconvoluted into three peaks located at 398.4, 400.2, and 401.4 eV, which are attributed to pyridinic-<i>N</i>, pyrrolic-<i>N</i>, and N–C═O bond, suggesting the doped N species in the carbon matrix (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> C 1s spectra further confirm the existence of the N in this composite which is derived from the precursor of urea (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e). These results can also explain the relatively lower intensity ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of the NVPFC-NS sample, which might be attributed to the structural changes of carbon caused by the increase in disorder and the influence of N doping. The N-doped carbon is more favorable to conductivity and can provide more active sites or defects for sodium storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The XPS spectra of F 1s and V 2p of NVPFC-NS are also supplied to confirm the element valance states. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>, the dominant peak at 683.2 eV can be assigned to F 1s, which is associated with the V–F bond. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a> shows the V 2p XPS core level of V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks with binding energies located at 517.3 and 524 eV, respectively, demonstrating the trivalent V in NVPFC-NS. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of the NVPFC-NS and NVPF-NP are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f. The NVPFC-NS material displays a type-IV isotherm of mesoporous characteristic with a Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area up to 28.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, which is larger than that of NVPF-NP (16.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). The pore-size distribution curves of both samples determined by the BJH method are provided. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, the pore size distribution results confirm again of their mesoporous properties. The pore-size distribution of NVPFC-NS centered around 3.87 nm is more uniform than that of NVPF-NP. In addition, NVPF-NP also has micropores. The relatively high surface area and mesoporous structures of NVPFC-NS are believed to be advantages of providing more active sites and an easy pathway for electrolyte penetration, facilitating the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> at high-rate charge/discharge operations.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b display the SEM images of NVPFC-NS and show the as-prepared sample has a 3D cross-linked structure constructed by interconnected nanosheets. Generally, the electrode materials with 3D architectures composed of 2D nanosheets present improved performance, which is conducive to obtaining high power by shortening diffusion paths and energy density by increasing loading per unit area. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The higher magnification SEM image of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c also displays that the nanosheets are interconnected with each other with macropores between them. Macroporous structure yields superior performance with high power density, which can serve as Na<sup>+</sup>/electron transfer shortcuts in dense structure and lead to rapid response to fluctuant current densities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The SEM and TEM images of NVPF-NP are also provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, and the SEM images show that the NVPF-NP is composed of nanoparticles with slight agglomeration. TEM images display the nanoparticles agglomerate together matched well with its SEM result.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) SEM image and its magnified images of (b) and (c), TEM image, (d) HRTEM image(e), and SAED image (f), (g) dark-field image of (d) and the corresponding elemental mappings of C, V, N, and F for NVPFC-NS, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07897&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The TEM image of NVPFC-NS, shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d, further confirms its unique 3D architecture constructed by continuous wrinkled nanosheets. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e shows the HRTEM image, presenting obvious lattice fringes with an interplanar distance of 0.151 nm and 0.452 nm, coinciding well with the lattice space of (335) and (200) facets of NVPF, respectively. In addition, amorphous carbon in situ coated on the nanosheets can also be seen at the edge, which ensures the rapid transfer of electrons across the nanosheets. The SAED image shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f certifies the existence of (200) facet with the other two crystal facets of (111) and (002) of NVPF. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g shows the dark-field image of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d and the corresponding elemental mappings of carbon (C), vanadium (V), nitrogen (N), and fluorine (F), indicating that all the elements are uniformly distributed on the nanosheets.</div><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate the performances of NVPFC-NS in the batteries, coin-type cells of the NVPFC-NS cathode of SIBs were assembled with sodium metal as the anode and tested in the voltage range from 2.3 to 4.5 V, where the specific capacities were calculated based on the total mass of the composites. Owing to the unique structure of NVPFC-NS with carbon in situ coated on 2D nanosheets, the NVPFC-NS electrode delivered a high initial capacity of 129 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with 97.6% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 0.5 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a), which shows the higher initial capacity and capacity retention compared with NVPF-NP. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b shows the rate performances of both samples with a 300-fold increase in current density (from 0.5 to 150 C). The NVPFC-NS electrode delivers an average discharge capacity of 132, 115, 110, 104, 93, and 65 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the corresponding rates of 0.5, 10, 20, 40, 60, and 100 C, respectively. Even at a high rate of 150 C (charge/discharge within 24 s), a highly reversible capacity of 51 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> could still be achieved. More importantly, when such an ultra-high current density of 150 C returns to 0.5 C, a remarkable specific capacity of 130 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> could turn back as well, because these in situ carbon-coated nanosheets with 3D integrated connected networks could not only achieve highly effective, continuously short and ultrafast pathways of both electrons and Na<sup>+</sup> but also buffer the variation of volume originated from the repeated extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. However, the sample of NVPF-NP without carbon coating exhibits a very inferior rate performance.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Na-storage properties of the NVPFC-NS nanocomposite in half cells. (a) Cycling stabilities of both samples at 0.5 C and their rate performances from 0.5 to 150 C (b,c) Voltage profiles for NVPFC-NS under different rates. (d) Comparison of the rate capability of NVPFC-NS with those of other NVPF/C composite electrodes previously reported. (e) Cycling performance at 10 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07897&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The charge and discharge profiles of NVPFC-NS under different rates are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c. Three distinct charge and discharge plateaus located at 3.4/3.2, 3.7/3.6, and 4.2/4.1 V are observed at 0.5 C corresponding to reversible extraction/insertion of two sodium ions. Note that the first two plateaus could be ascribed to Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation from Na (2) sites by a two-step process, while the higher potential at about 4.2 V is attributed to the (de)intercalation of the other Na<sup>+</sup> from Na(1) sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> Besides, as the rate increased, though presenting an obvious IR drop due to increased inner resistance of the cell, the voltage profiles of NVPFC-NS still show clear plateaus at all rates from 0.5 to 150 C, also confirming its superior rate performance and ultrafast kinetics of extraction/insertion.</div><div class="NLM_p">On basis of a comprehensive summary (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d), to the best of our knowledge, this is the best rate capability among recently reported NVPF/C composites for SIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref25 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref25 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 16, 17, 25 and 39 to 44">(16,17,25,39−44)</a> further demonstrating its advancement for ultrafast Na storage. Remarkably, the cycling performance of NVPFC-NS is outstanding as well. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e, the sample attains a reversible discharge capacity of 102 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the completion of 500 cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention of 93% relative to the first cycle, maintaining almost 100% Coulombic efficiency, highlighting the advantages of this in situ carbon-coated nanosheets-constructed 3D cross-linked cathode material in cycling performance. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a> shows the TEM and HRTEM images of the NVPFC-NS sample after 500 cycles at 10 C. Although the surface of NVPFC-NS has some cracks due to the variation of volume induced by Na<sup>+</sup> continuous insertion/extraction processes. The overall 2D nanosheet structure still exists, benefitting from the carbon in situ coated on the active material surface as the skeleton of the material preventing the collapse of the 2D structure. Besides, the HRTEM image shows that the active material still retains excellent crystalline after a long cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">To more deeply understand the remarkable high-rate performance of this cathode material, the CV was performed with different scan rates from 0.1 to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for NVPFC-NS, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a. The three pairs of redox peaks (O1/R1, O2/R2, and O3/R3) correspond to reversible extraction/insertion of two sodium ions, which is in good agreement with their charge/discharge profile results. The well-defined three pairs of redox peaks still exist even when the sweep speed increases to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, indicating rapid kinetics of the electrochemical reaction. The redox pseudocapacitance-like contribution in the NVPFC-NS sample was also analyzed by researching the kinetics (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b). The degree of capacitive effect can be qualitatively analyzed according to the relationship between measured current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) from the CV curves: <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, the value of <i>b</i> is calculated from the slope of the linear plot of log <i>i</i> versus log <i>ν</i>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> It is well known that for a diffusion-controlled process, b approaches 0.5, while for a surface capacitance-dominated process <i>b</i> is close to 1.0. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The calculated <i>b</i>-values of these three pairs of redox peaks are all around 0.7, close to 0.5, suggesting a diffusion-controlled process of sodium (de)intercalation for NVPFC-NS. The slightly higher <i>b</i>-values might be attributed to the fact that in situ carbon-coated nanosheets can provide fast transfers for Na<sup>+</sup> ions and electrons, which ensures an outstanding high rate performance of the NVPFC-NS electrode. CV curves at different scan rates and b values at different potentials of the NVPF-NP electrode are also provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>, at lower scan rates, three pairs of redox peaks are obviously presented in CV curves, corresponding to its typical redox reaction potentials. With the increase of scan rates, the peaks gradually widen and the polarization gradually increases, suggesting poor electrochemical reaction kinetics. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/suppl_file/am2c07897_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a> reveals the calculated b values, which are close to 0.5, suggesting a diffusion-controlled process.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/medium/am2c07897_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curves at different scan rates of the NVPFC-NS electrode. (b) <i>b</i> values at different potentials. (c,d) Corresponding relationship between the peak current (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>) and the square root of the scan rate (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07897/asset/images/large/am2c07897_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07897&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c,d, the good linear relationships for plots of peak current (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>) versus square root of the scan rate (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) also confirm that the sodium (de)intercalation reactions are controlled by a diffusion process. For the semi-infinite and finite diffusion, the peak current is proportional to the square root of the scan rate and can be expressed by the classical Randles–Sevcik equation: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a><i>I</i><sub><i>p</i></sub> = 2.69 × 10<sup>5</sup><i>n</i><sup>3/2</sup><i>AD</i><sup>1/2</sup><i>Cv</i><sup>1/2</sup>, where <i>I</i><sub><i>p</i></sub> is the peak current, <i>A</i> corresponds to the active area in cm<sup>2</sup>, <i>D</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, <i>n</i> represents the number of transferred electrons, <i>v</i> is the scanning rate in V s<sup>–1</sup>, and <i>C</i> is the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> in mol cm<sup>–3</sup>. The calculated <i>D</i> values of NVPFC-NS are 2.07 × 10<sup>–8</sup> (O1), 1.118 × 10<sup>–7</sup> (O2), 1.116 × 10<sup>–7</sup> (O3), 2.25 × 10<sup>–8</sup> (R1), 8.02 × 10<sup>–8</sup> (R2), and 9.6 × 10<sup>–8</sup> (R3), which are almost 2 order of magnitude higher than those reported previously by the same the calculation method (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>), demonstrating that the rational design of in situ carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> nanosheets not only shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer path but also improves the electronic conductivity during the charging/discharging process.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> Values of Similar Cathode Materials Reported in the Open Literature</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table char="×">2.07&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–8</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="×">1.118&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–7</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char"
